       continue to be the backbone of the economy. Tourism and the public service are the mainstay of the economy of Tobago, though authorities have begun to diversify the island.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The bulk of tourist arrivals on the islands are from the United States.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The country is also a recognised transhipment point for illegal narcotics, with the cocaine distribution from the South American continent to the United States Eastern seaboard. With the most recent seizure of $100 million US dollar shipment by United States Authorities on 17 January 2014.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">
        Politics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Politics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Politics of Trinidad and Tobago">
        Politics of Trinidad and Tobago
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:POS_Redhouse.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1544" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/POS_Redhouse.JPG/220px-POS_Redhouse.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/POS_Redhouse.JPG/330px-POS_Redhouse.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/POS_Redhouse.JPG/440px-POS_Redhouse.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:POS_Redhouse.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Red_House_(Trinidad_and_Tobago)" title="The Red House (Trinidad and Tobago)">
          The Red House
         </a>
         , Trinidad and Tobago's Parliament Buildings, 2008 (undergoing renovations)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Trinidad and Tobago is a
       <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">
        republic
       </a>
       with a
       <a href="/wiki/Two-party_system" title="Two-party system">
        two-party system
       </a>
       and a
       <a href="/wiki/Bicameralism" title="Bicameralism">
        bicameral
       </a>
       parliamentary system based on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Westminster_System" title="Westminster System">
        Westminster System
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">
        head of state
       </a>
       of Trinidad and Tobago is the
       <a href="/wiki/President_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="President of Trinidad and Tobago">
        President
       </a>
       , currently
       <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Carmona" title="Anthony Carmona">
        Anthony Carmona
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">
        head of government
       </a>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="List of Prime Ministers of Trinidad and Tobago">
        Prime Minister
       </a>
       , currently
       <a href="/wiki/Keith_Rowley" title="Keith Rowley">
        Keith Rowley
       </a>
       . The President is elected by an
       <a href="/wiki/Electoral_college" title="Electoral college">
        Electoral college
       </a>
       consisting of the full membership of both houses of
       <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Parliament of Trinidad and Tobago">
        Parliament
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_Minister" title="Prime Minister">
        Prime Minister
       </a>
       is elected from the results of a general election which takes place every five years. The President is required to appoint the leader of the party who in his opinion has the most support of the members of the House of Representatives to this post; this has generally been the leader of the party which won the most seats in the previous election (except in the case of the
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_general_election,_2001" title="Trinidad and Tobago general election, 2001">
        2001 General Elections
       </a>
       ). Tobago also has its own elections, separate from the general elections. In these elections, members are elected and serve in the Tobago House of Assembly.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Parliament of Trinidad and Tobago">
        Parliament
       </a>
       consists of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Senate_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Senate of Trinidad and Tobago">
        Senate
       </a>
       (31 seats) and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="House of Representatives of Trinidad and Tobago">
        House of Representatives
       </a>
       (41 seats).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The members of the Senate are appointed by the president. Sixteen Government Senators are appointed on the advice of the Prime Minister, six Opposition Senators are appointed on the advice of the
       <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(Trinidad_and_Tobago)" title="Leader of the Opposition (Trinidad and Tobago)">
        Leader of the Opposition
       </a>
       and nine Independent Senators are appointed by the President to represent other sectors of civil society. The 41 members of the House of Representatives are elected by the people for a maximum term of five years in a "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plurality_voting_system" title="Plurality voting system">
        first past the post
       </a>
       " system.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Political_culture">
        Political culture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Political culture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_President%27s_House_Trinidad.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/The_President%27s_House_Trinidad.jpg/220px-The_President%27s_House_Trinidad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/The_President%27s_House_Trinidad.jpg/330px-The_President%27s_House_Trinidad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/The_President%27s_House_Trinidad.jpg/440px-The_President%27s_House_Trinidad.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_President%27s_House_Trinidad.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/President%27s_House,_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="President's House, Trinidad and Tobago">
          President's House, Trinidad
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       From 24 December 2001 to 24 May 2010, the governing party has been the
       <a href="/wiki/People%27s_National_Movement" title="People's National Movement">
        People's National Movement
       </a>
       (PNM) led by
       <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Manning" title="Patrick Manning">
        Patrick Manning
       </a>
       ; the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parliamentary_opposition" title="Parliamentary opposition">
        Opposition
       </a>
       party was the
       <a href="/wiki/United_National_Congress" title="United National Congress">
        United National Congress
       </a>
       (UNC) led by
       <a href="/wiki/Basdeo_Panday" title="Basdeo Panday">
        Basdeo Panday
       </a>
       . Another recent party was the
       <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_People_(Trinidad_and_Tobago)" title="Congress of the People (Trinidad and Tobago)">
        Congress of the People
       </a>
       , or COP, led by
       <a href="/wiki/Winston_Dookeran" title="Winston Dookeran">
        Winston Dookeran
       </a>
       . Support for these parties appears to fall along ethnic lines with the PNM consistently obtaining a majority of Afro-Trinidadian vote, and the UNC gaining a majority of Indo-Trinidadian support. COP gained 23% of the votes in the 2007 general elections but failed to win a seat. Prior to 24 May 2010, the PNM held 26 seats in the House of Representatives and the UNC Alliance (UNC-A) held 15 seats, following elections held on 5 November 2007.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sampson_Nanton_interviews_former_Prime_Minister_of_the_Republic_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago,_Basdeo_Panday_in_1997.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="461" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Sampson_Nanton_interviews_former_Prime_Minister_of_the_Republic_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago%2C_Basdeo_Panday_in_1997.jpg/220px-Sampson_Nanton_interviews_former_Prime_Minister_of_the_Republic_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago%2C_Basdeo_Panday_in_1997.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Sampson_Nanton_interviews_former_Prime_Minister_of_the_Republic_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago%2C_Basdeo_Panday_in_1997.jpg/330px-Sampson_Nanton_interviews_former_Prime_Minister_of_the_Republic_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago%2C_Basdeo_Panday_in_1997.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Sampson_Nanton_interviews_former_Prime_Minister_of_the_Republic_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago%2C_Basdeo_Panday_in_1997.jpg/440px-Sampson_Nanton_interviews_former_Prime_Minister_of_the_Republic_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago%2C_Basdeo_Panday_in_1997.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sampson_Nanton_interviews_former_Prime_Minister_of_the_Republic_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago,_Basdeo_Panday_in_1997.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Basdeo_Panday" title="Basdeo Panday">
          Basdeo Panday
         </a>
         became the first Indo-Trinidadian prime minister in 1995.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       After two and a half years, Prime Minister Patrick Manning dissolved Parliament in April 2010, and called a general election on 24 May 2010. After these general elections, the new governing coalition is the People's Partnership led by Kamla Persad-Bissessar. Persad-Bissessar and "the People's Partnership" wrested power from the Patrick Manning-led PNM, taking home 29 seats to the PNM's 12 seats, based on preliminary results.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Business_culture">
        Business culture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Business culture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       It is estimated that the hidden economy represents 20–30% of measured GDP.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Within the Trinidadian and Tobagonian business structure illicit activities and licit activities work side by side, with many business and political organisations being funded by institutionalised drug smugglers. Within Trinidad and Tobago there are two main drug cartels, the Syrian/Lebanese drug cartels and the Indo Trinidadian drug cartels. The Syrian Lebanese drug cartels are the longest tenured drug cartel on the islands, having ridden the wave of cocaine exportation from the 1970s to the current day. The Syrian Drug Cartels control the vast sway of financing and business interest on the island and exhibit monopolistic tendencies which limit free market policies in insurance, health, finance, heavy and light manufacturing, and land distribution.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">
        Foreign relations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Foreign relations of Trinidad and Tobago">
        Foreign relations of Trinidad and Tobago
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Modern Trinidad and Tobago maintains close relations with its
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">
        Caribbean
       </a>
       neighbours and major North American and European trading partners. As the most industrialised and second-largest country in the English-speaking Caribbean, Trinidad and Tobago has taken a leading role in the
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Community" title="Caribbean Community">
        Caribbean Community
       </a>
       (CARICOM), and strongly supports CARICOM economic integration efforts. It also is active in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Summit_of_the_Americas" title="Summit of the Americas">
        Summit of the Americas
       </a>
       process and supports the establishment of the
       <a href="/wiki/Free_Trade_Area_of_the_Americas" title="Free Trade Area of the Americas">
        Free Trade Area of the Americas
       </a>
       , lobbying other nations for seating the Secretariat in
       <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Spain" title="Port of Spain">
        Port of Spain
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       As a member of CARICOM, Trinidad and Tobago strongly backed efforts by the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
        United States
       </a>
       to bring political stability to
       <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">
        Haiti
       </a>
       , contributing personnel to the Multinational Force in 1994. After its 1962 independence, Trinidad and Tobago joined the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">
        United Nations
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">
        Commonwealth of Nations
       </a>
       . In 1967, it became the first Commonwealth country to join the
       <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_American_States" title="Organization of American States">
        Organization of American States
       </a>
       (OAS). In 1995, Trinidad played host to the inaugural meeting of the
       <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Caribbean_States" title="Association of Caribbean States">
        Association of Caribbean States
       </a>
       and has become the seat of this 35-member grouping, which seeks to further economic progress and integration among its states.
      </p>
      <p>
       In international forums, Trinidad and Tobago has defined itself as having an independent voting record, but often supports US and
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        EU
       </a>
       positions.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Military">
        Military
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Military">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Defence_Force" title="Trinidad and Tobago Defence Force">
        Trinidad and Tobago Defence Force
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T%26T_Port_of_Spain-1-tonal.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1315" data-file-width="1996" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/T%26T_Port_of_Spain-1-tonal.jpg/220px-T%26T_Port_of_Spain-1-tonal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/T%26T_Port_of_Spain-1-tonal.jpg/330px-T%26T_Port_of_Spain-1-tonal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/T%26T_Port_of_Spain-1-tonal.jpg/440px-T%26T_Port_of_Spain-1-tonal.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T%26T_Port_of_Spain-1-tonal.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Coast Guard vessel in action
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trinidad_and_Tobago_Coast_Guard.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Coast_Guard.jpg/220px-Trinidad_and_Tobago_Coast_Guard.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Coast_Guard.jpg/330px-Trinidad_and_Tobago_Coast_Guard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Coast_Guard.jpg/440px-Trinidad_and_Tobago_Coast_Guard.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trinidad_and_Tobago_Coast_Guard.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Trinidad and Tobago Coast Guard members during a practice exercise at Staubles Bay in
         <a href="/wiki/Chaguaramas,_Trinidad" title="Chaguaramas, Trinidad">
          Chaguaramas
         </a>
         for the arrival of
         <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">
          Prince Charles
         </a>
         in 2008
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Trinidad and Tobago Defence Force is the military organisation responsible for the defence of the twin island Republic of Trinidad and Tobago. It consists of the
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Regiment" title="Trinidad and Tobago Regiment">
        Regiment
       </a>
       , the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Coast_Guard" title="Trinidad and Tobago Coast Guard">
        Coast Guard
       </a>
       , the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_Force_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Air Force of Trinidad and Tobago">
        Air Guard
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Defence_Force#Defence_Force_Reserves" title="Trinidad and Tobago Defence Force">
        Defence Force Reserves
       </a>
       . Established in 1962 after Trinidad and Tobago's independence from the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
        United Kingdom
       </a>
       , the Trinidad and Tobago Defence Force is one of the largest military forces in the English-speaking Caribbean.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Its mission statement is to "defend the sovereign good of The Republic of Trinidad and Tobago, contribute to the development of the national community and support the State in the fulfillment of its national and international objectives". The Defence Force has been engaged in domestic incidents, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Jamaat_al_Muslimeen_coup_attempt" title="Jamaat al Muslimeen coup attempt">
        1990 Coup Attempt
       </a>
       , and international missions, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Mission_in_Haiti" title="United Nations Mission in Haiti">
        United Nations Mission in Haiti
       </a>
       between 1993 and 1996.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">
        Administrative divisions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Trinidad is split into 14
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regional_Corporations_and_Municipalities_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Regional Corporations and Municipalities of Trinidad and Tobago">
        regional corporations and municipalities
       </a>
       , consisting of 9 regions and 5 municipalities, which have a limited level of autonomy. The various councils are made up of a mixture of elected and appointed members. Elections are due to be held every three years, but have not been held since 2003, four extensions having been sought by the government. The island of Tobago is governed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Tobago_House_of_Assembly" title="Tobago House of Assembly">
        Tobago House of Assembly
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <div class="center">
       <div class="thumb tnone">
        <div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Regional_corporations_and_municipalities_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg">
          <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="938" data-file-width="1000" height="469" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Regional_corporations_and_municipalities_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg/500px-Regional_corporations_and_municipalities_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Regional_corporations_and_municipalities_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg/750px-Regional_corporations_and_municipalities_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Regional_corporations_and_municipalities_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg/1000px-Regional_corporations_and_municipalities_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg.png 2x" width="500"/>
         </a>
         <div class="thumbcaption">
          <div class="magnify">
           <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Regional_corporations_and_municipalities_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg" title="Enlarge">
           </a>
          </div>
          Regional corporations and municipalities of Trinidad and Tobago
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="International_and_regional_relationships">
        International and regional relationships
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: International and regional relationships">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Organisation_of_American_States">
        Organisation of American States
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Organisation of American States">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Trinidad and Tobago is one of the 35 states which has ratified the OAS charter and is a member of the Organisation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oas.org_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-oas.org-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Charter of the Organisation of American States was signed in Bogota in 1948 and was amended by several protocols which were agreed to in different countries. The naming convention which is used with respect to the naming of the protocols is to include in the name of the Protocol the name of the city and the year in which the Protocol was signed, such as Cartagena de Indias in 1985, Managua 1993.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Trinidad and Tobago entered into the Inter-American system in 1967.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oas.org_54-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-oas.org-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">
        Economy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Economy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Economy of Trinidad and Tobago">
        Economy of Trinidad and Tobago
       </a>
      </div>

       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">

        </a>

         A proportional representation of Trinidad and Tobago's exports
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Refinerypointeapierre.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Refinerypointeapierre.JPG/220px-Refinerypointeapierre.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Refinerypointeapierre.JPG/330px-Refinerypointeapierre.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Refinerypointeapierre.JPG/440px-Refinerypointeapierre.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Refinerypointeapierre.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The oil refinery at
         <a href="/wiki/Pointe-%C3%A0-Pierre" title="Pointe-à-Pierre">
          Pointe-à-Pierre
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Point_Lisas.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="500" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Point_Lisas.jpg/220px-Point_Lisas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Point_Lisas.jpg/330px-Point_Lisas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Point_Lisas.jpg/440px-Point_Lisas.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Point_Lisas.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Point Lisas Port and Industrial Estate,
         <a href="/wiki/Couva" title="Couva">
          Couva
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Trinidad and Tobago is the most developed nation and one of the wealthiest in the Caribbean and is listed in the top 40 (2010 information) of the 70
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GNI_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GNI (PPP) per capita">
        high-income countries
       </a>
       in the world. Its gross national income per capita of US$20,070
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (2014 gross national income at Atlas Method) is one of the highest in the Caribbean.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In November 2011, the
       <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">
        OECD
       </a>
       removed Trinidad and Tobago from its list of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_countries" title="Developing countries">
        developing countries
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Trinidad's economy is strongly influenced by the petroleum industry. Tourism and manufacturing are also important to the local economy. Tourism is a growing sector, although not as proportionately important as in many other Caribbean islands. Agricultural products include citrus and cocoa.
      </p>
      <p>
       Recent growth has been fuelled by investments in liquefied natural gas (LNG), petrochemicals, and steel. Additional petrochemical, aluminium, and plastics projects are in various stages of planning. Trinidad and Tobago is the leading Caribbean producer of oil and gas, and its economy is heavily dependent upon these resources but it also supplies manufactured goods, notably food, beverages, and cement, to the Caribbean region.
      </p>
      <p>
       Oil and gas account for about 40% of GDP and 80% of exports, but only 5% of employment. The country is also a regional financial centre, and the economy has a growing trade surplus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-back_22-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-back-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The expansion of
       <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_LNG" title="Atlantic LNG">
        Atlantic LNG
       </a>
       over the past six years created the largest single-sustained phase of economic growth in Trinidad and Tobago. It has become the leading exporter of LNG to the United States, and now supplies some 70% of US LNG imports.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Trinidad and Tobago has transitioned from an oil-based economy to a natural gas based economy. In 2007, natural gas production averaged 4 billion cubic feet per day (110,000,000 m
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       /d), compared with 3.2
       <span style="margin-left:0.2em">
        ×
        <span style="margin-left:0.1em">
         10
        </span>
       </span>
       <s style="display:none">
        ^
       </s>
       <sup>
        6
       </sup>
       cu ft/d (91,000 m
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       /d) in 2005. In December 2005, the Atlantic LNG's fourth production module or "train" for liquefied natural gas (LNG) began production. Train 4 has increased Atlantic LNG's overall output capacity by almost 50% and is the largest LNG train in the world at 5.2 million tons/year of LNG.
      </p>
      <p>
       Trinidad and Tobago, in an effort to undergo economic transformation though diversification formed
       <a href="/wiki/InvesTT" title="InvesTT">
        InvesTT
       </a>
       in 2012 to serve as the country's sole investment promotion agency. This agency is aligned to the Ministry of Trade and Industry and is to be the key agent in growing the country's non-oil and gas sectors significantly and sustainably.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Trinidad and Tobago's infrastructure is good by regional standards.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">
          <span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (August 2009)">
           original research?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Piarco_International_Airport" title="Piarco International Airport">
        international airport
       </a>
       in Trinidad was expanded in 2001. There is an extensive network of paved roads with several good four and six lane highways including one controlled access expressway. The Ministry of Works estimates that an average Trinidadian spends about four hours in traffic per day.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Emergency services are reliable, but may suffer delays in rural districts.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Private hospitals are available and reliable.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Utilities are fairly reliable in the cities.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Some areas, however, especially rural districts, still suffer from water shortages.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Communications_Infrastructure">
        Communications Infrastructure
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Communications Infrastructure">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Trinidad and Tobago has a well developed communications sector. The telecommunications and broadcasting sectors generated an estimated TT$5.63 billion (US$0.88 billion) in 2014, which as a percentage of GDP equates to 3.1 percent. This represented a 1.9 percent increase in total revenues generated by this industry compared to last year. Of total telecommunications and broadcasting revenues, mobile voice services accounted for the majority of revenues with TT$2.20 billion (39.2 percent). This was followed by Internet services which contributed TT$1.18 billion or 21.1 percent. The next highest revenue earners for the industry were Fixed Voice services and Pay TV services whose contributions totalled TT$0.76 billion and TT$0.70 billion respectively (13.4 percent and 12.4 percent). International voice services was next in line, generating TT$0.27 billion (4.7 percent) in revenues. Free-to Air radio and television services contributed TT$0.18 billion and TT$0.13 billion respectively (3.2 percent and 2.4 percent). Finally, other contributors included “other revenues” and “leased line services” with earnings of TT$0.16 billion and TT$0.05 billion respectively, with 2.8 percent and 0.9 percent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There are several providers for each segment of the telecommunications market. Fixed Lines Telephone service is provided by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TSTT" title="TSTT">
        TSTT
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cable_%26_Wireless_Communications" title="Cable &amp; Wireless Communications">
        Cable &amp; Wireless Communications
       </a>
       operating as
       <a href="/wiki/Flow_(brand)" title="Flow (brand)">
        FLOW
       </a>
       ; cellular service is provided by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TSTT" title="TSTT">
        TSTT
       </a>
       (operating as
       <a href="/wiki/Bmobile" title="Bmobile">
        bmobile
       </a>
       ) and
       <a href="/wiki/Digicel" title="Digicel">
        Digicel
       </a>
       whilst internet service is provided by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TSTT" title="TSTT">
        TSTT
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Flow_(brand)" title="Flow (brand)">
        FLOW
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Digicel" title="Digicel">
        Digicel
       </a>
       , Green Dot and Lisa Communications.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">
        Transport
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Transport">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Transport in Trinidad and Tobago">
        Transport in Trinidad and Tobago
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CRHUBH_intersection.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1296" data-file-width="1936" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/CRHUBH_intersection.JPG/220px-CRHUBH_intersection.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/CRHUBH_intersection.JPG/330px-CRHUBH_intersection.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/CRHUBH_intersection.JPG/440px-CRHUBH_intersection.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CRHUBH_intersection.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         An intersection of
         <a href="/wiki/Churchill%E2%80%93Roosevelt_Highway" title="Churchill–Roosevelt Highway">
          Churchill–Roosevelt Highway
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Uriah_Butler_Highway" title="Uriah Butler Highway">
          Uriah Butler Highway
         </a>
         , 2009
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The transport system in Trinidad and Tobago consists of a dense network of highways and roads across both major islands, ferries connecting Port of Spain with
       <a href="/wiki/Scarborough,_Tobago" title="Scarborough, Tobago">
        Scarborough
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/San_Fernando,_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="San Fernando, Trinidad and Tobago">
        San Fernando
       </a>
       , and international airports on both islands. The
       <a href="/wiki/Uriah_Butler_Highway" title="Uriah Butler Highway">
        Uriah Butler Highway
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Churchill_Roosevelt_Highway" title="Churchill Roosevelt Highway">
        Churchill Roosevelt Highway
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Sir_Solomon_Hochoy_Highway" title="Sir Solomon Hochoy Highway">
        Sir Solomon Hochoy Highway
       </a>
       links the nation together. Public transportation options on land are public buses, private taxis and minibuses. By sea, the options are inter-island ferries and inter-city water taxis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The island of Trinidad is served by
       <a href="/wiki/Piarco_International_Airport" title="Piarco International Airport">
        Piarco International Airport
       </a>
       located in
       <a href="/wiki/Piarco" title="Piarco">
        Piarco
       </a>
       . It was opened on 8 January 1931. Elevated at 17.4 metres (57 ft)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Above_sea_level" title="Above sea level">
        above sea level
       </a>
       it comprises an area of 680 hectares (1,700 acres) and has a runway of 3,200 metres (10,500 ft). The airport consists of two terminals, the North Terminal and the South Terminal. The older South Terminal underwent renovations in 2009 for use as a VIP entrance point during the 5th
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Summit_of_the_Americas" title="Summit of the Americas">
        Summit of the Americas
       </a>
       . The North Terminal was completed in 2001, and consists of
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       14 second-level aircraft gates with jetways for international flights, two ground-level domestic gates and 82 ticket counter positions.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caribbean_Airlines_Boeing_737-800_9Y-TAB_(8504733249).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2007" data-file-width="3013" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Caribbean_Airlines_Boeing_737-800_9Y-TAB_%288504733249%29.jpg/220px-Caribbean_Airlines_Boeing_737-800_9Y-TAB_%288504733249%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Caribbean_Airlines_Boeing_737-800_9Y-TAB_%288504733249%29.jpg/330px-Caribbean_Airlines_Boeing_737-800_9Y-TAB_%288504733249%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Caribbean_Airlines_Boeing_737-800_9Y-TAB_%288504733249%29.jpg/440px-Caribbean_Airlines_Boeing_737-800_9Y-TAB_%288504733249%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caribbean_Airlines_Boeing_737-800_9Y-TAB_(8504733249).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The state-owned
         <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Airlines" title="Caribbean Airlines">
          Caribbean Airlines
         </a>
         is the largest in the region.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Piarco International Airport was voted the Caribbean's leading airport for customer satisfaction and operational efficiency at the prestigious World Travel Awards (WTA),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       held in the Turks and Caicos in 2006.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2008 the passenger throughput at Piarco International Airport was approximately 2.6 million.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Piarco_International_Airport" title="Piarco International Airport">
        Piarco International Airport
       </a>
       is the seventh busiest airport in the Caribbean and the third busiest in the English-speaking Caribbean, after
       <a href="/wiki/Sangster_International_Airport" title="Sangster International Airport">
        Sangster International Airport
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lynden_Pindling_International_Airport" title="Lynden Pindling International Airport">
        Lynden Pindling International Airport
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       As of December 2006, nineteen international airlines operated out of Piarco and offered flights to twenty-seven international destinations.
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Airlines" title="Caribbean Airlines">
        Caribbean Airlines
       </a>
       , the national airline, operates its main hub at the Piarco International Airport and services the Caribbean, the United States, Canada and South America. The airline is wholly owned by the Government of Trinidad and Tobago. After an additional cash injection of US$50 million, the Trinidad and Tobago government acquired the Jamaican airline
       <a href="/wiki/Air_Jamaica" title="Air Jamaica">
        Air Jamaica
       </a>
       on 1 May 2010, with a 6–12 month transition period to follow.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Airlines" title="Caribbean Airlines">
        Caribbean Airlines
       </a>
       , the national and state-owned airline of Trinidad and Tobago, is the largest in the Caribbean. After the acquisition of the now defunct Air Jamaica, it became the largest airline and was voted as the Caribbean's leading airline.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Island of Tobago is served by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.N.R._Robinson_International_Airport" title="A.N.R. Robinson International Airport">
        A.N.R. Robinson International Airport
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Crown_Point,_Tobago" title="Crown Point, Tobago">
        Crown Point
       </a>
       . This airport has regular services to North America and Europe. There are regular flights between the two islands, with fares being heavily subsidised by the Government
       <a href="/wiki/Crown_Point,_Tobago" title="Crown Point, Tobago">
        .
       </a>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Creative_Industries">
        Creative Industries
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Creative Industries">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Government of Trinidad and Tobago has recognized the creative industries as a pathway to economic growth and development. It is one of the newest, most dynamic sectors where creativity, knowledge and intangibles serve as the basic productive resource. In 2015, the
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Creative_Industries_Company" title="Trinidad and Tobago Creative Industries Company">
        Trinidad and Tobago Creative Industries Company Limited (CreativeTT)
       </a>
       was established as a state agency under the Ministry of Trade and Industry with a mandate to stimulate and facilitate the business development and export activities of the Creative Industries in Trinidad and Tobago to generate national wealth, and, as such, the company is responsible for the strategic and business development of the three (3) niche areas and sub sectors currently under its purview –
       <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Music of Trinidad and Tobago">
        Music
       </a>
       , Film and Fashion.
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Music_Company" title="Trinidad and Tobago Music Company">
        MusicTT
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Film_Company" title="Trinidad and Tobago Film Company">
        FilmTT
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_Fashion_Company" title="Trinidad and Tobago Fashion Company">
        FashionTT
       </a>
       are the subsidiaries established to fulfil this mandate.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">
        Demographics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [

         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Demographics of Trinidad and Tobago">
        Demographics of Trinidad and Tobago
       </a>
      </div>

       <span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_Centres_and_Towns">

       <span class="mw-editsection">

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">
        Ethnic groups
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Ethnic groups">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The ethnic composition of Trinidad and Tobago reflects a history of conquest and immigration.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While the earliest inhabitants were of Amerindian heritage, since the 20th century the two dominant groups in the country were those of South Asian and of African heritage.
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-Trinidadian_and_Tobagonian" title="Indo-Trinidadian and Tobagonian">
        Indo-Trinidadian and Tobagonians
       </a>
       make up the country's largest ethnic group (approximately 37.6%). They are primarily descendants from indentured workers from India, brought to replace freed African slaves who refused to continue working on the sugar plantations. Through cultural preservation some residents of Indian descent continue to maintain traditions from their ancestral homelands.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Afro-Trinidadians_and_Tobagonians" title="Afro-Trinidadians and Tobagonians">
        Afro-Trinidadians and Tobagonians
       </a>
       make up the country's second largest ethnic group, with approximately 36.3% of the population identifying as being of African descent. People of African background were brought to the island as slaves as early as the 16th century. 24.4% of the population identified in the 2011 census as being of "mixed" ethnic heritage. There are small but significant minorities of people of
       <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">
        Amerindian
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Trinidadian" title="White Trinidadian">
        European
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Trinidadian_and_Tobagonian" title="Chinese Trinidadian and Tobagonian">
        Chinese
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">
        Arab
       </a>
       descent.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">
        Languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       English is the country's official language (the local variety of standard English is
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidadian_English" title="Trinidadian English">
        Trinidadian English
       </a>
       or more properly, Trinidad and Tobago Standard English, abbreviated as "TTSE"), but the main spoken language is either of two
       <a href="/wiki/English-based_creole_languages" title="English-based creole languages">
        English-based creole languages
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidadian_Creole" title="Trinidadian Creole">
        Trinidadian Creole
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Tobagonian_Creole" title="Tobagonian Creole">
        Tobagonian Creole
       </a>
       ), which reflects the Amerindian, European, African, and Asian heritage of the nation. Both creoles contain elements from a variety of African languages; Trinidadian English Creole, however, is also influenced by French and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antillean_Creole_French" title="Antillean Creole French">
        French Creole
       </a>
       (Patois).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Spanish is estimated to be spoken by around 5% of the population
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and has been promoted by recent governments as a "first foreign language".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A majority of the early
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_indenture_system" title="Indian indenture system">
        Indian immigrants
       </a>
       spoke
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Hindustani#Trinidadian_Hindustani" title="Caribbean Hindustani">
        Trinidadian Hindustani
       </a>
       , which is a form of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhojpuri" title="Bhojpuri">
        Bhojpuri
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Awadhi" title="Awadhi">
        Awadhi
       </a>
       dialect of
       <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">
        Hindustani (Hindi-Urdu)
       </a>
       , which later became the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
         lingua franca
        </a>
       </i>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-Trinidadian_and_Tobagonian" title="Indo-Trinidadian and Tobagonian">
        Indo-Trinidadian and Tobagonians
       </a>
       . Attempts are being made to preserve the
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Hindustani#Trinidadian_Hindustani" title="Caribbean Hindustani">
        Trinidaian Hindustani
       </a>
       language in the country, including the promotion of
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-Caribbean_music" title="Indo-Caribbean music">
        Indo-Trinidadian and Tobagonian musical forms
       </a>
       called
       <a href="/wiki/Pichakaree" title="Pichakaree">
        Pichakaree
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Chutney_music" title="Chutney music">
        Chutney
       </a>
       , which are typically sung in a mixture of
       <a href="/wiki/Trinidadian_English" title="Trinidadian English">
        English
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Hindustani#Trinidadian_Hindustani" title="Caribbean Hindustani">
        Trinidadian Hindustani
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The indigenous languages were
       <a href="/wiki/Yao_language_(Trinidad)" title="Yao language (Trinidad)">
        Yao
       </a>
       on Trinidad and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karina_language" title="Karina language">
        Karina
       </a>
       on Tobago, both Cariban, and
       <a href="/wiki/Shebaya_language" title="Shebaya language">
        Shebaya
       </a>
       on Trinidad, which was Arawakan.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">
        Religion
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trinidad_and_Tobago&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Religion">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waterloo_Temple,_Trinidad.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4128" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Waterloo_Temple%2C_Trinidad.jpg/220px-Waterloo_Temple%2C_Trinidad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Waterloo_Temple%2C_Trinidad.jpg/330px-Waterloo_Temple%2C_Trinidad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Waterloo_Temple%2C_Trinidad.jpg/440px-Waterloo_Temple%2C_Trinidad.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waterloo_Temple,_Trinidad.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Temple_in_the_Sea" title="Temple in the Sea">
          Temple in the Sea
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="barbox tright" style="overflow-x: auto;">
       <div style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:200px; background: white;">
        <table style="border-collapse:collapse; width:100%;">
         <tr style="background:#ddd">

           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
            <a href="#cite_note-72">
             [72]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </th>
         </tr>
         <tr style="font-size:88%; height:4px;">
          <td style="padding:0 4px;">
           Religion
          </td>

          </td>

           <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
            Christianity
           </a>
          </td>
          <td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">

           55.3%
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; min-width: 8em;">
           <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">
            Hinduism
           </a>
          </td>
          <td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;">
